<div id="review_1485585104" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1485585104" />
    <a title="Ridhika Khanna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37988055-ridhika-khanna"><img alt="Ridhika Khanna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456744764p2/37988055.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1485585104?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ridhika Khanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ridhika Khanna" href="/user/show/37988055-ridhika-khanna">Ridhika Khanna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">all nature lovers and also people who are into hiking/climbing.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37988055-ridhika-khanna?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37988055-ridhika-khanna?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37988055-ridhika-khanna?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37988055-ridhika-khanna?shelf=tibet">tibet</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37988055-ridhika-khanna?shelf=mountains">mountains</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37988055-ridhika-khanna?shelf=travel">travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37988055-ridhika-khanna?shelf=wildlife">wildlife</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37988055-ridhika-khanna?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37988055-ridhika-khanna?shelf=nepal">nepal</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1485585104" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3248773141768420302">Actual Rating: 4.5<br/><br/>This book has been a very refreshing and a peaceful read.<br/><br/>In the beginning, Mr. Pico Iyer introduces this book as a mystical book which takes you on a journey in Himalayas where you can feel each and every blister on your feet. <br/>It is a big thing to say and I was actually apprehensive on reading it as the introducer had set high expectations for me. What if it is not that good? What if it is just like any other travel book?<br/><br/>However, I decided to read it. It took me a few chapters </span>
  <span id="freeText3248773141768420302" style="display:none">Actual Rating: 4.5<br /><br />This book has been a very refreshing and a peaceful read.<br /><br />In the beginning, Mr. Pico Iyer introduces this book as a mystical book which takes you on a journey in Himalayas where you can feel each and every blister on your feet. <br />It is a big thing to say and I was actually apprehensive on reading it as the introducer had set high expectations for me. What if it is not that good? What if it is just like any other travel book?<br /><br />However, I decided to read it. It took me a few chapters to get into this book but after that it was wonderful. I liked the writing prose of the writer. He literally takes you on this journey along with him. I liked the way he describes landscape. He has an observant eye and a knack for details. He is a very descriptive writer and beautifully paints a picture of the magnificent Himalaya. Not only this, I could actually picture myself taking a hike while reading. <br /><br />Also, he has a good understanding of mountain terminologies and used them effectively.<br /><br />I liked his idea of presenting this book in a chronological order like diary entries. It made his writing more streamlined. I have read other books from this genre with broken writing. <br /><br />There are some extra ordinary moments in this book. I liked his interactions with the sherpas, potters and villagers. It is highly commendable how he has been able to understand and describe life in Nepal Himalayas. I could feel how tough, merciless yet simple life is in that part of the world. <br />I liked his mysticism mixed with his present climbing situations. He didnt go overboard with it and knew when to restrain himself. <br /><br />I especially liked how he was able to reflect on his own life while climbing the Himalaya. I would like to relate one of my own experiences. <br />My first encounter with the pure/true Himalaya was during a mountaineering course in Uttarkashi in 2014. As I was in a tough physical course, I had to carry my own equipment and pitch my own tent. Carrying the same load day in and day out, shifting camps and pitching tents everyday were part of routine. Climbing is definitely not all that easy and glamorous as it looks on the outside. The serene vistas provided some respite and also a reward for such a grueling task. On reaching camps, I always took out some time to sit in front of a beautiful scenery. Without even realizing, I gave a lot of thought to life, about what I had done, what I hadn't, what I should have, what I want in life, about past experiences/relationships, about future aspects, about present, about Himalaya, about tough life here, about so many innumerable things. It helped me to give me more clarity about myself, my life and how I want to live it. Eventually after a month's training when I went back home, I was a better person than I was a month ago. <br /><br />I could relate a lot with Matthiessen when he reflected on his life. I guess it happens with everyone. In such extreme situations and such beautiful vistas one is bound to experience something un-imaginable. I liked that part in the book a lot.<br /><br />I liked the details and explanations regarding blue sheep or bharal. It was an interesting read. It was good to know that all this climbing and struggle eventually was fruitful. <br /><br />I also liked his quest on snow leopard.<br /><br />Why not 5 stars? Peter Matthiessen is a meandering writer and sometimes goes off track or sometimes goes into too much of details. Not like George Martin though. He sticks to the point and makes sure he hits home. :)<br />Just sometimes, I felt a few things could have been skipped.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3248773141768420302" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_45320988" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/45320988" />
    <a title="Lars Guthrie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/757602-lars-guthrie"><img alt="Lars Guthrie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1199816668p2/757602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/45320988?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lars Guthrie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lars Guthrie" href="/user/show/757602-lars-guthrie">Lars Guthrie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer45320988" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2911897514819121817">Cliches become cliches because of their truth. So noting that the journey is more important than the destination fits here, in a book whose title refers to the author's quest to view the snow leopard in its Himalayan habitat during a perilous late fall journey to the remote Dolpo region of Nepal, an area so far from the rest of the world that the author's traveling companion notes the total absence of machine sound, even the engine noise of a plane. Matthiessen fails to see the snow leopard, but</span>
  <span id="freeText2911897514819121817" style="display:none">Cliches become cliches because of their truth. So noting that the journey is more important than the destination fits here, in a book whose title refers to the author's quest to view the snow leopard in its Himalayan habitat during a perilous late fall journey to the remote Dolpo region of Nepal, an area so far from the rest of the world that the author's traveling companion notes the total absence of machine sound, even the engine noise of a plane. Matthiessen fails to see the snow leopard, but gains something else. When he is finally able to look at himself in a mirror, he sees a different person than the one he was. Like all good travel writers, he gives more than just descriptions (although his descriptions of the mountains and their people are stunning). After the death of his wife, Matthiessen searches for meaning and significance and finds it in his own insignificance. He cites Reb Nachman of Bratslav: 'As the hand held before the eye conceals the greatest mountain, so the little earthly life hides from the glance the enormous lights and mysteries of which the world is full, and he who can draw it away from before his eyes, as one draws away a hand, beholds the great shining of the inner worlds.' At the end of the voyage, he is not the only different one, for his reader can now see someone at least a little newer in the mirror. I like the way Ursula LeGuin rephrases the chestnut: 'It is good to have an end to journey towards; but it is the journey that matters in the end.' <br />									 <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2911897514819121817" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2742770192" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15308514078839120985">Finally done with this. It was the first time for me to read a book by Peter Matthiessen and I'm afraid I didn't like it too much. First of all, this book was chosen by my book club for our visit to Nepal. However, most of the book is really about Tibetan culture. I imagine Nepalese culture to be slightly if not more different than Tibetan. Secondly, the author underwent to a difficult journey. His wife just died of cancer and he left his young 8 year old son for a dangerous adventure climbing t</span>
  <span id="freeText15308514078839120985" style="display:none">Finally done with this. It was the first time for me to read a book by Peter Matthiessen and I'm afraid I didn't like it too much. First of all, this book was chosen by my book club for our visit to Nepal. However, most of the book is really about Tibetan culture. I imagine Nepalese culture to be slightly if not more different than Tibetan. Secondly, the author underwent to a difficult journey. His wife just died of cancer and he left his young 8 year old son for a dangerous adventure climbing the Himalayas. He didn't speak Tibetan, which made communication with the sherpas difficult. He wanted to find peace and the meaning of life among the Tibetan Buddhists but instead he keeps bickering bitterly about the sherpas. I didn't find him very respectful and didn't understand the choices he made. Lastly, the book didn't hold my attention well. I found myself always reaching out for other books, but also feeling out of obligation to finish this for the book club journey.<br /><br /><i>"And then, almost everywhere, clear and subtle illumination that lent magnificence to life and peace to death was overwhelmed in the hard glare of technology. Yet that light is always present, like the stars of noon. Man must perceive it if he is to transcend his fear of meaninglessness, for no amount of "progress" can take its lace. We have outsmarted ourselves, like greedy monkeys, and now we are full of dread."</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="15308514078839120985" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2472502925" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2472502925" />
    <a title="Mary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/909198-mary"><img alt="Mary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461957170p2/909198.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2472502925?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary" href="/user/show/909198-mary">Mary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2472502925" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6887678370997230770">While I will never hike or climb in the Himalayas, this travel novel provides details on the beauties and hardships of such an adventure. But this work is more than a travelogue, it is a journey of spiritual quest and discovery for the author with the elusive Snow Leopard serving as a token of his goal. <br/><br/>The book takes the form of a daily journal recounting the terrain, weather, vegetation, wildlife, and meals of the small party. Accompanied by a world renowned expert on the Himalayan Blue Sheep,</span>
  <span id="freeText6887678370997230770" style="display:none">While I will never hike or climb in the Himalayas, this travel novel provides details on the beauties and hardships of such an adventure. But this work is more than a travelogue, it is a journey of spiritual quest and discovery for the author with the elusive Snow Leopard serving as a token of his goal. <br /><br />The book takes the form of a daily journal recounting the terrain, weather, vegetation, wildlife, and meals of the small party. Accompanied by a world renowned expert on the Himalayan Blue Sheep, the scientific goal of the trip is to climb high enough to watch the rut. <br /><br />The author however worries throughout about becoming snowed in and not making it back home for the holidays. Recently widowed, he has a young son he misses. <br /><br />Well worth reading for the journey and the insights and lessons that the author an adherent of Zen experiences.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6887678370997230770" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6471120926225021302">It’s taking me forever to finish THE SNOW LEOPARD by Peter Matthiessen - a brilliant and beautiful masterwork, a meditation on Zen Buddhism, a travelogue and a moving memoir. Reading only a few pages at a time because I don’t want it to end. Ever.<br/></span>

<div id="review_33723450" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33723450" />
    <a title="Ade Bailey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/863801-ade-bailey"><img alt="Ade Bailey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1207383586p2/863801.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33723450?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ade Bailey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ade Bailey" href="/user/show/863801-ade-bailey">Ade Bailey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/863801-ade-bailey?shelf=biography-autobiography">biography-autobiography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33723450" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17197779991809602676">This is a much revered book which I was looking forward to reading. It’s not a climbing book, though the long trek two man expedition contains its fair share of ardours that you’s expect to find at Himalayan altitudes. And actually, of course, the expedition is one of many men, the porters and Sherpas too. I would mention only that the attitude to the latter sometimes left me with a bad taste. With Matthiessen was zoologist George Schaller, and the prime purpose was to study the Himalayan bharal</span>
  <span id="freeText17197779991809602676" style="display:none">This is a much revered book which I was looking forward to reading. It’s not a climbing book, though the long trek two man expedition contains its fair share of ardours that you’s expect to find at Himalayan altitudes. And actually, of course, the expedition is one of many men, the porters and Sherpas too. I would mention only that the attitude to the latter sometimes left me with a bad taste. With Matthiessen was zoologist George Schaller, and the prime purpose was to study the Himalayan bharal, or blue sheep. The author was drawn by the hope of seeing a snow leopard, and because of his immersion in Zen Buddhism. His wife had died a year before, and he reminisces and reflects on this, and also on the older children he had left behind (at school or college), and the youngest, Alex, aged eight who he left to be looked after by the family of friends of his. These reflections form one strand of the book.<br />	Another, as mentioned, is the detailed account of the journey, its hardships, relationships. A third strand, which I do recommend is information and descriptions of animals from snow leopards to Yeti, also of botanical matters. Similarly, anyone interested in it will find useful information about Tibetan/Himalayan Buddhism. And, of course, there is the travelogue which passes through villages, towns and meetings with ‘interesting people’.<br />	Had I read the book thirty years ago I would rave about it. But to be honest I am somewhat disappointed and weary of it. At one stage I wish that the book had lost at least a third of its weight, that he hadn’t bothered when, after all, “Frustration at the paltriness of words drives me to write, but there is more of Shey in a single sheep hair… than in all these notes; to strive for permanence in what I think I have perceived is to miss the point.”<br />	Matthiessen is a gifted writer, but I became irritated that he has a flatness of style that is equalled only by the negative psychologigcal states he constantly bemoans. He’s had, he tells us, quite a spiritual journey which involved hallucinogenic drugs as well as Zen training. I felt sometimes more that I was wading through darkness (‘Pitched past pitch of grief/….O the mind has mountains; cliffs of fall/ Frightful, sheer, no-man-fathomed’, G.M. Hopkins in bleaker mode) than among the pure white at-oneness up in the spiritual roof of the world (“Snow, mountains, more than sea or sky, serve as a mirror to one’s own true being, utterly still, utterly clear, a void, an Emptiness without life or sound that carries in Itself all life, all sound.”: this is a typical bit of Matthiessen prose, and the book is full of it.) Matthiessen certainly ‘knows’ his Buddhism and will fill you in throughout your journey with gaps in your education about the Way. The trouble is, and I acknowledge that it may be my trouble, my reading of it, the moments of spiritual insight come across less as awed and more as awful: turgid, formulaic, highly conceptualised, derirative, stock responsive, ultimately sentimental. There is no lightness of touch in his ‘Zen’. It cloys and tastes bad.<br />	‘Expect nothing,’ he reflects on the words of his Zen teacher, as he struggles to ‘live in the moment’, after a certain failure of goal. And on his way back to the twentieth century he moans that, “ I have neglected my children and done myself harm, and there is no way back…. I will perform the motions of parenthood, my work, with friendships, my Zen practice, but all hopes, acts and travels have been blighted. I look forward to nothing.” Rationalising this, he is able as anybody could who had a mind to, that this is the meaning of it all, after all. Soon, less than a day later, he is proclaiming how wonderful his life is. <br />	There is an underlying gloom to Matthiessen’s persona. I hope that he went on in life to discover the centrality of compassion to Buddhism and was able to overcome his attachments and revulsions, and his mood swings. <br />	Yet, I have no doubt that for many this is or will be a wonderful book. Despite my misgivings, which I have admitted may be very peculiar, I nevertheless commend it. I just think some readers may need to skim and leave him to it when he gets moody.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17197779991809602676" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2298785824" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2298785824" />
    <a title="R K" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25898891-r-k"><img alt="R K" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596208633p2/25898891.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2298785824?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="R K" class="user" itemprop="url" name="R K" href="/user/show/25898891-r-k">R K</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2298785824" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9461374919662385381">DNF<br/>Terrible <br/>I wanted to gouge my eyes out<br/><br/>Oh boy. I gave up on this book. An alternative title to this book should be: “The Snow Leopard: As elusive in this book as in real life”. <br/><br/>For a book that was supposed to be a travelogue into the world of the snow leopard, there were endless pages of dense reflections on Buddhism. I know Peter Matthiessen is supposed to be a student of Buddhism, but if I wanted to read a book about Buddhism, I wouldn’t have picked up a book called “The Snow Leopard”. <br/><br/>Every</span>
  <span id="freeText9461374919662385381" style="display:none">DNF<br />Terrible <br />I wanted to gouge my eyes out<br /><br />Oh boy. I gave up on this book. An alternative title to this book should be: “The Snow Leopard: As elusive in this book as in real life”. <br /><br />For a book that was supposed to be a travelogue into the world of the snow leopard, there were endless pages of dense reflections on Buddhism. I know Peter Matthiessen is supposed to be a student of Buddhism, but if I wanted to read a book about Buddhism, I wouldn’t have picked up a book called “The Snow Leopard”. <br /><br />Every 5 seconds we would drift off into some gabble about Buddhism that made no sense. Sometimes I feel that the concepts of Buddhism aren’t so hard to grasp, but scholarly scriptures make it harder. What I mean is, I often wondered if Matthiessen himself knew what he was talking about. The point where I gave up was when he was talking about doing LSD while “reveling in the lessons and scriptures of Buddhism”. I understand that during the 60-70, associating drugs to eastern philosophy was a thing, but putting that in a book isn’t going to make me believe that the person is serious about learning <s>(or even knows what their saying). </s><br /><br />The book follows Matthiessen and his partner “GS”, and I did not warm up to either men. Matthiessen made no sense with his paralogues and GS was just an awful person. Matthiessen tried to understand GS for the readers, but you can’t wrap a knife in pretty wallpaper and call it a day. GS was just rude and the excuse “he doesn’t know how to act around people” is no excuse. He didn’t know how to act around people but he could certainly pass judgement. Speaking of which, both men provided “insight” on the environment and people of Nepal, Tibet, and India, and you can tell how flawed this insight was. They could comment on how sad Indians looked and how there was poverty and how the environment wasn’t being cared for. They extended this insight to Pakistan and even said that the US army should make their soldiers plant trees in order to increase the 3% forest coverage in Pakistan since they didn’t seem to be doing anything else. Again, they were passing judgement and inaccurate information. The book was published in 1978, and probably written earlier. Asia, at the time, was going through a lot so I’m just going to summarize (very roughly). India just received its independence (around 31 years prior) and had to go through a bloody partition, displacement of people and culture, and wars. It had to bring its country out of the state colonization left it in, and you don’t go through something as harrowing as that without mass suffering. It’s sad, and unfortune, and devastating, and affects everyone to some extent. I know I shouldn’t get riled over something that was written in the 70s. It’s not like people were as aware of things as we are today, so we shouldn’t really judge. But seeing as these parts were the only bits that made any coherence, and then seeing how misinformed these perspectives were, naturally made me zoom in on them. <br /><br />It was horrible and I quit. I just wanted a book on snow leopards. I went to the back of the book and saw that they put an index. Maybe the publishers also realized that people who picked this up might be wondering where the snow leopards were too. <br /><br />What did you think? Leave a comment!<br />©thescientificdetective2019<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9461374919662385381" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('VQVn+xbpYdjnIMuj7ypRm2TgVxQtOl72ddTRk/JLlDm5s2/xiyFaZk1OgMvL0p+EmZ+0RLQxSV4T0gBoFWDEBA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kB8VoPebEI8JAbN4nzjCpHSqXNazDIogKyagIsH1G+d8qR2qalMrMaNv+BC7wAy7idW/hioHnYhNIHHZJt5L2g==')})
  }

Set in almost uninhabitable regions of Central Australia, The Songlines asks and tries to answer these questions: Why is man the most restless, dissatisfied of animals? Why do wandering people conceive the world as perfect whereas sedentary ones always try to change it? Why have the great teachers—Christ or the Buddha—recommended the Road as the way. to salvation? Do we agree with Pascal that all man\'s troubles stem from his inability to sit quietly in a room?\n\nWe do not often ask these questions today for we commonly assume that living in a house is normal and that the wandering life is aberrant. But for more than twenty years Chatwin has mulled over the possibility that the reverse might be the case.\n\nPre-colonial Australia was the last landmass on earth peopled not by herdsmen, farmers, or city dwellers, but by hunter-gatherers. Their labyrinths of invisible pathways across the continent are known to us as Songlines or Dreaming Tracks, but to the Aboriginals as the tracks of their ancestors—the Way of the Law. Along these &quot;roads&quot; they travel in order to perform all those activities that are distinctively human—song, dance, marriage, exchange of ideas, and arrangements of territorial boundaries by agreement rather than force.\n\nIn Chatwin\'s search for the Songlines, Arkady is an ideal friend and guide: Australian by birth, the son of a Cossack exile, with all the strength and warmth of his inheritance. Whether hunting kangaroo from a Land Cruiser, talking to the diminutive Rolf in his book-crammed trailer, buying drinks for a bigoted policeman (and would-be writer), cheering as Arkady\'s true love declares herself (part of The Songlines is a romantic comedy), Chatwin turns this almost implausible picaresque adventure into something approaching the scale of a Greek tragedy.\n\nThe life of the Aboriginals stands in vivid contrast, of course, to the prevailing cultures of our time. And The Songlines presents unforgettable details about the kinds of disputes we know all too well from less traumatic confrontations: over sacred lands invaded by railroads, mines, and construction sites, over the laws and rights of a poor people versus a wealthy invasive one. To Chatwin these are but recent, local examples of an eternal basic distinction between settlers and wanderers. His book, devoted to the latter, is a brilliant evocation of this profound optimism: that man is by nature not a bellicose aggressor but a pacific, song-creating, adaptive species whose destiny is to       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/6975.Peter_Matthiessen">About Peter Matthiessen</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/6975.Peter_Matthiessen"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1408550935p3/6975.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/6975.Peter_Matthiessen">Peter Matthiessen
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
611
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14848421909222803103">Peter Matthiessen is the author of more than thirty books and the only writer to win the National Book Award for both non-fiction (The Snow Leopard, in two categories, in 1979 and 1980) and fiction (Shadow Country, in 2008). A co-founder of The Paris Review and a world-renowned naturalist, explorer and activist, he died in April 2014.</span>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1824318-the-snow-leopard">Quotes from The Snow Leopard</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;The secret of the mountain is that the mountains simply exist, as I do myself: the mountains exist simply, which I do not. The mountains have no "meaning," they are meaning; the mountains are. The sun is round. I ring with life, and the mountains ring, and when I can hear it, there is a ringing that we share. I understand all this, not in my mind but in my heart, knowing how meaningless it is to try to capture what cannot be expressed, knowing that mere words will remain when I read it all again, another day.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/615696-the-secret-of-the-mountain-is-that-the-mountains-simply">90 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;And only the enlightened can recall their former lives; for the rest of us, the memories of past existences are but glints of light, twinges of longing, passing shadows, disturbingly familiar, that are gone before they can be grasped, like the passage of that silver bird on Dhaulagiri.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/293719-and-only-the-enlightened-can-recall-their-former-lives-for">55 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/1824318-the-snow-leopard">More quotes&hellip;</a>
